B005LAII08|4.0|So what is there to say that hasn't been said? All the reviews for this film have nearly universally claimed that the animation is fantastic, as one would expect, and it is indeed. The scenery is gorgeous, the 3D really helps the beautiful visuals pop and provides depth, the character designs are distinctive (if not slightly reminiscent of Dreamworks How to Train Your Dragon). Any animation fan will enjoy watching this film.<br /><br />As for the film overall, the story is charming, if not also quite familiar, and there is certainly enough humor and funny sounding pronunciations (the Scottish accents are purposefully hammed up at times) to keep children engaged. One caution up front, I would advise against taking toddlers, or extremely timid children, as there is a frightening bear, seen for a much more tame moment in the previews, who has several less tame scenes scattered throughout the film. Another caution for readers of the review, I am going to strongly hint at the major plot point of the film, I won't come right out and say it but I will say that by now most will have pieced it together from previews and other reviews, so this review shouldn't spoil anyone who hasn't figured it out already.<br /><br />All this said, I usually don't write reviews to simply regurgitate what has been said already, so I do have a point here I would like to make. The difference between this film and a true transcendent classic is that this film does nothing new or truly unique (well perhaps one thing, but we'll get to that). It is excellent to see a female heroine (especially one this strong) but the very notion of putting a female up front is something that has been done purposefully before, so even that cannot be said to make this film unique. I'm sure many, especially some mothers, will adore this film and they have every right to, it is charming in many respects. A great many more people will enjoy this film moderately and put it in their collection to rewatch, but this film will never be as great or as remembered as it could have been, and as an animation/Pixar fan that leaves me somewhat disappointed.<br /><br />The previews are deliberately vague regarding the actual story of this film. The opening is clear enough, a princess doesn't want to marry and butts heads with her mother so she goes in search of a spell to change her fate... it all seems familiar but is bold in its honesty and execution. However, from there I found the film became less Brave and more like what I will call Mama Bear, somewhat of a spinoff take on Disney's Brother Bear. The film runs with this new conceit, dropping what I deemed to be the more interesting story of the princess's efforts to win her own hand and convince her mother to change tradition, but never goes anywhere with it other than the usual predictable places. Predictability isn't a bad thing, it is often comforting and in many ways is even expected from any film, let alone a family film. But as a Disney Pixar film one would have thought they'd push the boundaries here or at least have the story be incredibly well told. Ultimately, the 'Mama Bear' idea just isn't mined to its full potential, though that doesn't stop it from being fun and having laughs.<br /><br />Overall the film, which admittedly had a troubled production, feels like a pastiche of earlier efforts by Disney and even some of their rival studios. There is even a random witch character thrown into the mix but then she disappears never to return or be mentioned again. A lot of elements in the film are that way, they appear, play their part in the plot and then disappear or vanish until they may or may not be needed again. There are few strong through lines. More evidence to this is that the only villain truly presented here seems to be stubbornness (and perhaps that scary bear who waltzes in and out of the plot with little purpose), which is again a potentially interesting angle but it doesn't fully develop and things are wrapped up tooeasily in that regard without any sacrifice and surprisingly little effort. The film also seems unable to decide whether it wants to be classic fairy tale (i.e. Disney's traditional formula) or take the true Pixar/Dreamworks route and have a modern take (i.e. Toy Story, Shrek, etc.), as such much of the film feels in a definite time and place but then certain scenes have modern references thrown in.<br /><br />Now back to finding praise for the film, the stubborn characters are the princess, and her mother the queen. Both want what they want and desire for the other to see their point of view (sound familiar to any parents of adolescents?). The story does do an excellent job of portraying the trials of a mother and daughter relationship, at least from my experiences as an observer. The teenage princess really goes for the throat and the mother truly loses her control in one crucial moment and it all feels heartwrenchingly true to life. The one original thing I praise about the film is that mother-daughter relationship, but ironically at the same time it feels too realistic and thus out of place in a movie that tones everything else down. Another positive note is that neither the mother nor the daughter ultimately gives into the other but instead a wonderful scene shows how both have come to understand the other and they find a common ground, which is much better than simply having a stubborn parent realize they were wrong (you know, little mermaid style).<br /><br />I do recommend this film to animation fans, children kindergarten and up, or even for mothers and their adolescent daughters. It is well made and extremely well animated but overall as a 23 year old male film fan who adores Pixar (and Disney even more so) I have to say this film reminded me of Disney's Brother Bear, which was unfortunately only moderately successful. While I personally love that film and feel it brings together so many themes so wonderfully (the Inuit culture, man vs. nature, family bonds, beautiful and unique settings) this film takes the conceit from that film and never plays it out as well as it could or with the originality one comes to expect from Pixar. Even a straight A student gets a B once and a while, I just wish this film had been as strong willed as its main character, picked one direction and been more... well, Brave.<br /><br />Still highly recommended, with less gags but definitely more depth than Madagascar 3, but far from a must see for the casual filmgoer.

B006Z7Z3S6|3.0|***This review contains spoilers***<br /><br />This movie has a good concept but a few issues stunted the potential. On the positive notes, a) I really liked the hotel setting, b)the concept of two youngish (Pat Healy is ~40) people having run of an old hotel in its last days for a ghost hunting project is awesome and something I'd love to do, c) acting is decent, and d) while clearly not entirely successful, the movie *tries* to avoid many horror movie cliches.<br /><br />On to the bad...<br /><br />Arguably the biggest problem was the character Luke -- in fact, in a  loathsome way, he's kind of thrust upon us as the real star of the show by virtue of his despicable and unfortunately enduring (not! endearing) personality.  There's technically only 2 key characters, Luke and Claire, and the Luke half is completely unlikeable.  It's clear Luke's character was intended to be snarky and endear little sympathy, but typically, we as an audience are satisfied to see that kind of character either get killed off or redeem himself at the end.  Unfortunately, neither happens with Luke in this film, as he's not only the last man standing of the two key characters, but his unlikeable personality traits endure till the very end.  Early on in the film, most of us were probably calling BS on Luke's account of seeing the ghost, but somehow not able to remember any details, so it was no surprise when later on after running away that he finally admitted to Claire he made it all up.  The guy is a jerk, a coward, and a liar.  And at the end of this movie, we have two people dead, one of which is clearly a suicide and the other clearly...? Ok, we are first led to believe Claire died purely as a result of being pursued by the corpse of the dead man and the ghost of Madeline.  Personally, I was thinking the police should have started looking at Luke as the primary suspect, because after all, how was he going to explain that ghosts were responsible?  Well, the movie has the answer -- she dropped her asthma inhaler and not having that to prevent an attack is what did her in.  That was pretty weak.<br /><br />Another issue is that the movie was good at staying just interesting enough to keep me tagging along in hopes of being rewarded with some great scare scenes and finding out what the real story behind the supposed ghosts are, however, the movie ultimately drags things out too long for a disappointing payoff.  And for all that time spent with very little happening, we never get to understand what the deal is with the 3 spirits that are supposed to be there and why the ghost of Madeline, and eventually the corpse of the old man, were malevolent toward Claire.  Was it due to anger and bitterness held over from the trauma and heartbreak from when they were alive?  Was it because the other spirits, who have no backstory, were evil and either forcing the other spirits to carry out bad deeds or perhaps masquerading as those otherwise benign entities?  We never find out and these aren't the kind of \\"good\\" questions to have at the end of a movie.  Don't get me wrong though, most of my favorite horror movies are of the \\"less is more\\" type and a slow build-up is great -- when done well.<br /><br />And then there was the mother and her son staying at the hotel briefly.  What was their purpose in the context of this film?  Did they need one?  The answer is no, but I would have liked the inclusion of these characters to have been utilized in a way that had some kind of impact on the major plot of the story.  For example, they could have either been focused on in a way as to be a red herring in the plot development of what was going on in the house (and they were not), or, the movie could have found a way to circle back to them toward the end, showing them as more key to the story then we were led to originally believe (and this did not happen).<br /><br />I think there's a good reason that this movie has no better than a 3-star average in the reviews here and that's where my rating is being placed as well.  Despite the issues I found with the movie and my \\"I would have done it this way\\" comments, I have to circle back to my notes on the positives of this movie as reasons enough to go as high as 3 stars and the fact that, in spite of it all, I'd recommend others give this movie a shot and come to their own conclusions -- I'd love to hear them.

0307352145|5.0|Teachers and parents need to read this book before engaging in a program to \\"fix\\" a quiet, shy or sensitive child.  Adults who have been criticized from childhood for not being social enough or aggressive enough will finally find in Susan Cain somebody who understands and affirms their temperament.  Cain is both an introvert and a passionate advocate for the quiet souls in American schools and businesses.  She includes stories of real introverts, past and present, so readers can learn from their struggles and achievements.<br /><br />Eleven well-researched chapters (50 pages of end notes) discuss the biology and psychology of temperament, offer practical advice for both introverts and extroverts about communicating with one another, and point out cultural differences (e.g. Asians value introverts while Americans marginalize them.) If this were not enough, Quiet also gives practical advice for structuring offices and classrooms so that introverted workers and students can think and create without being intimidated or overwhelmed. If you have been the victim of an open office floor plan or a \\"collaborative learning\\" classroom, Cain feels your pain!<br /><br />I appreciate finally finding someone who does not consider introversion a disorder to be \\"cured\\" but an alternate, equally valid way of thinking and working.  Susan Cain understands how I could once pretend to love the loud, back-slapping atmosphere of a consulting environment while inwardly wishing I could just go work in my office with the door closed--forever! I learned I'm not the only one who doesn't want to go to women's conferences where I have to play games, spill my guts to the whole class or engage in \\"team-building exercises.\\"  Should I be considered less spiritual if I prefer just a few close friends or would rather work in the kitchen or library than teach a large group?<br /><br />My one slight irritation was her use of Al Gore as one of her examples. B007I2BZIE|3.0|I have been a Rush fan since 1979 and first saw them in 1981. To give you an idea of my tastes, I actually preferred the keyboard years with Power Windows and Hold Your Fire, followed by the Farewell To Kings, Hemispheres, Permanent Waves, and Moving Pictures period. I would love to see them re-do 2112 with better vocals. I hated Vapour Trails and much of Counterparts.<br /><br />Being that I saw them play Caravan and BU2B on the last tour, I was not really looking forward to the \\"in your face\\" approach displayed on those two songs, which open up this new album. So I ordered 4 new releases by other artists from Amazon to go along with this one. The box of 5 new CD's arrived nine days after this was released, so I had time to read all of the polarized reviews. Of course, this new Rush CD got played first. I hate to admit it, but it was by far the least enjoyable out of the entire box. So what happened to my beloved band of Canadians?<br /><br />The first clue is the cover, with the clock showing the time at 9:12, which is 21:12 on the 24-hour clock.  Hmmm! A subtle hint from that concept album released about 40 years ago led me to first believe that this was planned as their masterpiece four decades later. Well, the concept is great! But musically, I think that they just tried too hard. Many have blamed the producer, but how can one do that when he was the same guy who did a great job on Snakes And Arrows? That argument of bad production just doesn't fly. The first five songs made me want to turn the damn thing off, just droning on and on, but The Anarchist and Carnies do have some good moments. Things lightened up with Halo Effect (a title of one of my own lyrics from two years ago, and dare I say, was better than Neil's version, even if the tone of mine was negative). I wound up finding the rest of this one likable, with my favorites being Headlong Flight, The Wreckers, Wish Them Well, and The Garden. Basically this album whips your tail in the beginning and then apologizes in the end by trying to tell you that everything will be okay.<br /><br />What else do I not like about this one? Well, I learned a new word called \\"steampunk\\", which describes all of the images on this one as well as their last release. Once again, this now qualifies them as \\"arena rock\\" instead of progressive rock, because this whole concept was done with the express intent of doing an over-the-top live show with all of these steampunk images. Yes, it can be quite trippy, but I am too old for all of the heavy foolishness trying to figure out what they really are trying to say. Heavy!!!? It so happens that I could care less for steampunk art and hidden meanings.<br /><br />I have a better idea for their next concept album. Based upon all of these polarized reviews from all of us old geezers (and a few young ones too), they can set the scene at Judge Roy Bean's Saloon in Langtry. Since the place only holds about a half dozen patrons at a time, Rush can have all of us old geezers take turns sitting down to have a few drinks in the place. Okay, so that is kind of a recycled version of them playing as the German Oompah band in the video on the last tour, but what the heck, everything is recycled these days, including Rush's own signature riffs. B006RUJST6|4.0|When Lake's father dies suddenly, her mother decides to move their family from familiar Texas to the small town in Michigan where she grew up. Independent, beautiful, and intelligent Lake falls quickly for her neighbor Will, and when their younger brothers become instant best friends, it seems like the perfect set up for a new start. However, when they discover a secret that could threaten the lives they are building, they begin to wonder if the stakes are just too high to risk it.<br /><br />Slammed starts strong with well written characters, unexpected twists and turns, and offers a solid narrative. Colleen Hoover manages to write Lake, Will, and Julia's vulnerabilities without making them tiresome or needy. She explores challenging situations that have no right answer, making the moral path the story goes down exciting, but also thought provoking. The book includes slam poetry and she uses visual effects such as boldface type to help place the reader at the event, almost as if you were sitting in the audience listening, making a technique that could come off as irritating or trite into something powerful and affecting. Additionally, the poems are great and the device isn't overused.<br /><br />Bottom line - I couldn't put it down. Definitely worth the price.

B000024Y9L|4.0|Well, there's Maiden fans and there's classic Maiden fans, and that's fine, but if you want 80s Maiden, don't expect it here - pick up the Dickinson solo albums from this era with Adrian Smith (Accident of Birth and Chemical Wedding), those have the classic Maiden sound with modern, drop-tuned metal touches.  What Maiden did in the 90s was - completely ignore the 90s and go their own way.  In fact, in the early 90s, they went for a classic Judas Priest, AC/DC, Deep Purple inspired version of Iron Maiden, while the late 90s albums (including this one) used a more subdued guitar tone (inspired, to my ears, by Rush, Queensryche, and mid-period Fates Warning, maybe?) with new singer Blaze Bayley having a deeper, croonier, moody vocal style more reminiscent of Dio (but less rock'n'roll).  It's a different sound, but it still is more Iron Maiden than it is anything else - it's not like the 90s mainstream rock direction taken by Metallica's \\"Load\\"s or Def Leppard's \\"Slang\\".<br />  \\"Virtual XI\\" is a little brighter and faster than the moody, dirgy \\"X Factor\\" of '95.  It kind of reminds me of Somewhere in Time - both due to the SciFi lyrical/art themes and the fact that both albums seem a little less dark and dangerous than the band's other works.  But, also, both albums still have the basic metalness of songwriting - I mean, Iron Maiden never goes into hair metal territory like Judas Priest's \\"Turbo\\", for example!  That said, \\"Angel & The Gambler\\" has kind of a classic rock feel (The Who come to mind) crossed with \\"Heaven Can Wait\\".  At 9+ minutes, I was hoping for a classic Maiden epic, but it mainly repeats the same line (\\"Don't you believe I'm your saviour\\" - but, without, to my understanding, the Christian overtones that line would normally imply) a million times while \\"bringing it down\\" and building it up again.  I think this is the kind of thing that should've been saved for the live version, on album it becomes too repetitive, leaving this a flawed song that I neverthelss rather like.  \\"The Clansman\\", however, IS a classic Maiden epic, and went over pretty well in live shows when Bruce rejoined.  \\"Como Estais Amigos\\" is kind of a ballad, but avoids the cliches of the typical power ballad (that is, it's a mellow metal song that's still musically interesting, rather than simply a boring soft rock song written by a metal band) and has a nice instrumental section in the middle.  \\"Futureal\\" is the fast, rocking opener and succeeds pretty nicely at this.  \\"When Two Worlds Collide\\" is pretty much a classic Maiden-style song, with the current \\"sound\\" of '98.  Overall, I like the songs, I like the sound - it's not the fast, dangerous, Iron Maiden of the 80s, but it's good music by a band that still contains many of the great musical elements that made Maiden stand out in the metal scene when they started.  I bought this when it first came out in 1998, and I've found it holds up well to repeated listens - it may not come out as often as \\"Powerslave\\" or \\"Brave New World\\", but it's a welcome change every now and then for this Iron Maiden fan.

0439023521|4.0|Unlike some readers, I had the pleasure of meeting this book after I saw the movie. And I have not read the 2 other books. Usually before I write a review, I make a point of reading all of the previous reviews of the book on this site. Unfortunately the sheer mass of these makes this impractical. So I might be stating views already given, but here goes.<br /><br />The most interesting aspect of what reviews I did read were those by people negative. Like KMR, who suggested that &#34;the premise ... lacks authenticity&#34; and &#34;the people of Panem are so resigned ... they never question it&#34; and &#34;in 74 years, not even one tribute has questioned the mandate&#34;. But look at our own unlamented North Korea. Under a stifling and near absolute dictatorship that harks back to 1945 when World War 2 ended 67 years ago. Where in the last 20 years a famine killed over a million people. Yet no rebellion. The 67 year Korean rule is not so far from the 74 years of the Hunger Games. Perhaps the reviewer should be more cognisant of actual history.<br /><br />Then there is the reviewer Pseudotriakis, who opined that &#34;the book is boring&#34;. Here the overwhelming mass of readers would absolutely disagree. As you can see by trolling the reviews, most found it gripping. And that is the verdict of the marketplace, based on the book's success. Plus, the movie was a faithful rendition of the book, as stated by the author herself. The movie was a smash for 2012. Which goes to show that the book was not boring.<br /><br />Further, Pseudotriakis ventures that &#34;Even when she is forced to kill, she feels little or no remorse&#34;... &#34;The author never lets her kill pre-emptively, only allowing her to act in self defense&#34;. The reviewer lacks enough introspection to see that he is largely answering his own question. The reason that Katniss feels no remorse is basically because she killed in self defense. Though I suggest that the author did this precisely to avoid the harder issue of how to depict the character if she had indeed killed several opponents, especially if this was done deliberately. If you read the book and think back slowly over the tournament events, you might get an extra appreciation for what Collins did and her skill as a writer. For all of the action and deaths, Katniss manages to segue aside. This lets her emerge at the end not as a hardened stone cold killer, and much easier for the reader to identify with.<br /><br />I suggest that overall the critical comments about the society or societies in the book inadvertently show the author's constraint. She built this out into 2 more books where she fleshed out those facets. Including from what I can gather in the blurbs, where indeed people rebel against Panem. If she had put these into the current book, it would have made for a far longer text. Keep in mind one practical factor. This first book was market speculation by the author, as it really is for any author. She did not know how well it would be received. Investing too much time in a huge tome that was all 3 novels would have been a big risk of her time. Likely too that the publisher would have baulked at backing it.<br /><br />Hey, if you liked this novel, there is another story about a death match that starts with 64 gladiators in a future world. Each paired with an opponent and in every round half the players die, until only 1 is left. Berserker's Planet by Fred Saberhagen. Not as successful in the marketplace. Mostly perhaps because the characters in that book exhibit far less empathy than Hunger Games. The biggest differences with Hunger Games are that those fighters enter the contest willingly and can be of any age. If the combat premise of Collins was interesting and you want more, try that book. It is also a reminder that Collins certainly did not invent the idea of a death match with only one winner. Surely there are other books of death matches, but the Saberhagen text is what I've read.<br /><br />Another book recommendation that will be more germane to some readers is the companion text about the filming of the movie, The Hunger Games: Official Illustrated Movie Companion. 0307341577|4.0|This is probably the most brutal novel I have ever read though granted it is my first foray into this kind of crime thriller. It is an excellent book though at times I felt I had to take a break and/or a shower to get out of the darkness. The novel deals with the brutal and seemingly easily solved and prosecuted murder of a family in Kansas in which there was only one survivor, the seven year old daughter Libby. Doubt of her brother's guilt leads Libby to investigate the crime and make contact with the jailed brother. The details of the murder are not glossed over. Many of the characters are so dysfunctional and not terribly likable but you are dragged to see that many of them are not what they seem and to grudgingly respect if not outright like them. It shines a light on the fact that we all try to do the best we can with our families, but that despite our best efforts, many people screw up royally or become royally screwed up.<br /><br />Flynn's writing is like none I have ever read. Her descriptions are incredible but extremely odd: objects compared to things you would have never imagined them to be compared to. There is a description of a street of shabby houses whose owners make somewhat of an effort to keep them up. These houses are compared to a group of less than average looking girls who get themselves sequined and made up to go out to a bar with the hope that by placing the one average looking girl in the middle of the group, all the group would look more attractive. So many details are conveyed in the smallest of barely mentioned details: the main character, who lost several toes and fingers to frostbite while she hid out from the killer who murdered her family, has a hand upon which no man will ever place a wedding ring. If that doesn't tell you everything about the character's lovability factor..... These details and description allow Flynn to effortlessly create complex, fully developed characters.<br /><br />Not every novel has to have a happy ending.This one certainly doesn't have clear happy ending, but as disturbing as this novel is, you want there to be an unequivocal hopeful ending. However this ending is open to interpretation and only one interpretation could give you a glimmer of hope. But I kind of like that it is open to interpretation despite my gut wish.

0307346617|4.0|When I first saw \\"World War Z\\" listed on NPR's top 100 Sci-fi and Fantasy Books list, I told myself, \\"One of those zombie gore-fests? Really? I'll never read that.\\" But this changed when my \\"zombie\\" friend convinced me it was more story than gore, much like \\"The Walking Dead\\" on AMC. He lent me the book and I promised to read it.<br /><br />It turns out that Max Brooks is a great story teller. He tells the story of mankind's ten year war against the zombies, presented as a series of interviews compiled by some unnamed character. Rather than concentrate on the gore and the gotta-kill-em as most zombie movies do, Max explores how humans react to the disaster.<br /><br />It begins with the story of a doctor who first came in contact with the disease. At first he's skeptical of the villagers' superstitious stories, but when he sees firsthand a boy's arm falling off without the boy screaming, he realizes this isn't an ordinary disease.<br /><br />As the epidemic expands, each country reacts differently. Some make stupid decisions, resulting in more casualties, while others are wiser. Some people shine as heroes, while others try to take advantage and earn money or power in creative ways.<br /><br />It's clear that Brooks has thought out what one should do in a zombie attack, but sometimes the science and logistics don't quite work out. For example, how is it the zombies only exist east of the Rockies in the USA? How exactly can one person survive the roads of a busy city full of zombies? But the story telling is so good, it's so easy to overlook these details.<br /><br />Each individual story is hit or miss. Most of them are hits involving an individual's story of survival. The misses usually cover something logistical and are usually short. One funny miss appears to exist only to jokingly refer to Brooks' own \\"Survival Guide.\\"<br /><br />Brooks does a decent job in speaking in different voices, though the tone of each interview is similar. He makes use of a lot of country-specific cliches. For example, a Korean story talks about some secret the North is hiding. A Japanese story talks about honor and involves one of those swords. The military people tend to drop F bombs, while scientists apologize for even getting close to cussing. These cliches are noticeable, but they work.<br /><br />My biggest complaint about the book is that the identity of each interviewee is withheld in the beginning and each time the reader must figure out who the person is. Sometimes this requires going back to reread earlier paragraphs. At the beginning of each interview, we're only given the place (not necessarily where the events occurred) and a short blurb from the interviewer describing the scenery. Had this been a real account of a real event, each interview heading would have included the person's name, and save the reader time spent on rereading.<br /><br />But I can see what drove Brooks' decision. There are a couple of stories that hinge on the interviewee's identity being revealed at the very end. But Max, did you really have to torture us through all the other stories where it would have really helped to know who was talking?<br /><br />Overall, I highly recommend this book. If you like \\"The Walking Dead\\", you'll like this book. And just like me, you'll probably choose your favorite story. Mine is the Chinese submarine one.ry-specific cliches. For example, a Korean story talks about some secret the North is hiding. A Japanese story talks about honor and involves one of those swords. The military people tend to drop F bombs, while scientists apologize for even getting close to cussing. These cliches are noticeable, but they work.<br /><br />My biggest complaint about the book is that the identity of each interviewee is withheld in the beginning and each time the reader must figure out who the person is. Sometimes this requires going back to reread earlier paragraphs. At the beginning of each interview, we're only given the place (not necessarily where the events occurred) and a short blurb from the interviewer describing the scenery. Had this been a real account of a real event, each interview heading would have included the person's name, and save the reader time spent on rereading.<br /><br />But I can see what drove Brooks' decision. There are a couple of stories that hinge on the interviewee's identity being revealed at the very end. But Max, did you really have to torture us through all the other stories where it would have really helped to know who was talking?<br /><br />Overall, I highly recommend this book. If you like \\"The Walking Dead\\", you'll like this book. And just like me, you'll probably choose your favorite story. Mine is the Chinese submarine one.

1451608659|4.0|I was excited to get a behind-the-scenes look into the life of an elite gymnast in this memoir. Moceanu takes us through her life, from her birth and early years, to her first gym, to her experiences training at the (in)famous Karolyi ranch, her family difficulties, the Olympics, and her life after the Olympics. She writes in an open and honest style, and has the ability to discuss some of the worst parts of her life with a mature outlook that lacks bitterness. Interspersed throughout the book are chapters on discovering and meeting a long-lost sister, who had been given up at birth by her parents.<br /><br />I appreciated learning much more about what it takes to train at the elite level and the uglier side of training with two well-known gymnastics coaches. When you watch gymnasts on television, they make everything look so easy that it's hard to remember all the sacrifices that are made to be an Olympic-gymnast, from training through injuries to giving up normal childhood food.  Moceanu's descriptions of the Karolyi's made a particular impression, and I admire her for getting through that experience. Additionally, I also found the story of her Jennifer, her long-lost sister, particularly inspiring - I would love to see a memoir by her as well! Finally, I liked the pictures that were included in the middle of the book, showing her family and some of her famous gymnastics moments.<br /><br />I would recommend this memoir to anyone who follows gymnastics or who is interested in athlete autobiographies.

1451648537|5.0|Everybody who reads this book is enthusiastic about the depth and frankness of the story it tells about Steve Jobs's life: how honest it is, since Jobs gave the author permission to write whatever he wanted to without Jobs's approval, even without Jobs reading the book. Indeed it calls out for such honesty, because Steve Jobs was a complex, fascinating, and extremely unpleasant person, who sustained obvious psychological damage from being given up for adoption by his birth parents--and also a genius.<br />Isaacson seems to go inside the story--he himself is a genius, in my opinion--telling about the numerous staff meetings in which Jobs regularly savaged his staff, calling their work \\"shit\\" and later on, taking credit for those ideas that survived. Jobs would have been a very difficult person to work with (or live with, naturally); but somehow he managed to surround himself with people who did his bidding and went on to produce ingenious products. He really did build an A team. Interestingly, Isaacson doesn't think Jobs was exceptionally smart; but, he says, Jobs was a genius, because he followed his intuition and it led to sensationally successful products--and the company, and the brand--and a cult following of Apple users.<br />The fact that Jobs was a sociopath makes the story all that much more interesting, because instead of rendering him an outcast, it helped propel him to greatness. I was gratified to read that he was allowed to attend my alma mater, Reed College, in his own outside-the-rules fashion, attending only the classes that interested him, not paying tuition, and leaving when it suited him.<br />I very much like Jobs's idea of the crossroads of technology and the humanities intersecting, and the aesthetic vision he pushed so hard, and successfully, to achieve. Even his impatience with mediocrity was understandable. But it's a shame he was such an unpleasant person. The conundrum is, were the two necessarily intertwined: the genius visionary with nearly-impossible standards, and the social tyrant?

There is a fix (but really, should we need to have to fix something like this?). After you start watching the show pull up the menu while the show is playing. Go to subtitles and turn on the English subtitles - not the English SDH subtitles. Then exit from the menu to the already playing show. Then pull up the menu AGAIN, go to subtitles, then choose no subtitles. Then you can exit the menu and only the foreign languages will be subtitled. This will have to be done any time you change discs.

0765316994|5.0|I could summarize the plot of this novel in a short paragraph, but I won't because if I did, you won't have the \\"Oh, that's different\\" experiences I had reading it, and I quite enjoyed them. I wouldn't want to ruin those for you.<br />At one level, this is obviously a parody of Star Trek, The Original Series, told from the perspective of the poor unfortunate crewmembers sent on away missions for the sole purpose of dying in interesting and dramatic ways to advance the plot. As such, it's a hoot. It has aliens, space travel, split realities, and inexplicable tech stuff. It also has additional layers that make it more than a comic ride through the galaxy aboard the Universal Union Spaceship 'Intrepid'. These layers provide depth and make this a solid, thought provoking read. But even with all the philosophical and existential brain-bending, it is still a lighthearted and charming book.<br />When I started reading this, it reminded me of the movie Galaxy Quest. Then it became more like Star Trek IV. Then it turned a corner and seemed a bit like the Thursday Next books (by Jasper Fforde) with maybe a touch of The Never Ending Story. (Yes, I confess to being something of a geek, but who else would be reading stuff like this?) My point is that there is more than one story being told in this novel. It's rather like a thought experiment in novel form. I loved it.<br />The story has interesting, likeable characters, witty dialogue, and a very Star Trek-like setting. But if you're looking for a mindless action adventure with a few jokes, this isn't for you. If you're looking for a simple slapstick parody of Star Trek, this isn't it. It is also nothing like Scalzi's Old Man Goes to War books (which are also very good) or even Fuzzy Nation (also a winner for me). Scalzi continues to grow as a writer, and this book proves it. I won't say it's better than his other books, but it is definitely different. What I will say is that I thought is was so good I wish I wrote it.

1593272782|5.0|I've had all three of Isogawa's Technic Idea books for a while now. After reading some of the reviews and comments here, I felt compelled to contribute some information to this discussion. The point I want to make is that these books are *Idea Books*, not *cookbooks*. You would have guessed that from the book title, but reading through the reviews here, it appears that this isn't obvious, so let me explain.<br /><br />But before I do, in Mr. Isogawa's own words: \\"This book is full of little seeds for ideas. It is you who will cultivate these seeds so they will grow into wonderful masterpieces.\\" This means that this book is full of ideas, not ready made recipes that you would find in a cookbook. There's no list of ingredients, and  also no step-by-step instructions to make everything. If you want full instructions, this may not be the right book for you.<br /><br />This book has numerous examples that show the essential principles of building LEGO vehicles. It also shows how you can use LEGO motors to make your vehicles move. And here's where \\"all those expensive rare components\\" come in. Truth is, you really don't need all these pieces.<br /><br />The best example is the use of motors. Pages 4-17 show you many working examples of motor powered vehicles. All of these vehicles do the same: move when you switch on the motor, so you really need to build only one of them. Now, all these vehicles illustrated on these pages use *6 different types* of LEGO motors (to be clear, only one motor per vehicle). The author has done this so that literally *anyone with any LEGO motor* can build this little car.<br /><br />So you really need just one motor. And once you've followed Mr. Isogawa's suggestions on how to mount your particular motor on a (any) LEGO construction, you're ready to add this motor to all of the other special vehicles in this book - even if it doesn't picture your motor. All motors do the same. Just plug and play.<br /><br />Clearly the same goes for wheels. Every wheel will work. And if your wheels are too big to fit the example car, just make the car bigger. After all, that's the point of this book: inspire you to build your own creations.<br /><br />While the author has kindly provided a parts list that show the pieces used in this book, I actually do not recommend to buy pieces based on this list. Rather, I would suggest that you buy one of the bigger LEGO Technic Sets, especially one with at least one motor. That's much more pieces for your money, plus you can build an awesome truck or bulldozer when you're done building wheeled wonders.<br /><br />And instead of going to Bricklink to hunt down rare pieces, search for \\"Peeron\\", which is a site that shows which pieces can be found in each LEGO set. Just enter the part number of the LEGO set and find what's in it. You can still buy that great looking truck, but be sure to use this site to check that it includes some gears, wheels and a motor so you're good to go to build the vehicles in this book.<br /><br />And as with any LEGO related product, a child may need a little guidance. A child won't read this review, but if you read it, just build something together with your kid and show them what I said here. B004XQO90O|2.0|*** This review may contain spoilers ***<br /><br /> The concept where a particular past juncture in time is re-visited in order to effect a change in future circumstances, was marvelously handled in the brilliant comedy-fantasy, 'Groundhog Day'. 'Source Code' utilizes a similar concept, but here it should basically be subsumed under the genre of Sci-fi Thriller. Unlike 'Groundhog Day', which repeatedly brings a time-tripping Bill Murray back to the past by sleight of hand, 'Source Code' provides a pseudo-scientific rationale, which proves in the end to be decidedly derivative of other time travel tales we've seen before.<br /><br />Our protagonist, former Army helicopter pilot, Colter Stevens, now severely injured and in a comatose state, is being used as part of a secret US Army project dubbed 'Source Code', to uncover the identity of an individual who bombed a Chicago commuter train, earlier that day. The creator of the project, Dr. Rutledge, and his assistant, Air Force Captain Goodwin, repeatedly keep sending Stevens back eight minutes prior to the bombing inside an experimental device which resembles a space capsule, in order to find out who planted the bomb so they can stop him from detonating an even more destructive dirty bomb in downtown Chicago, which the bomber has promised to do an hour later.<br /><br />Stevens learns that he's unable to change events in the future reality which Goodwin and Rutledge are a part of, and is only able to be an observer. The Source Code enables Stevens to enter the body of one of the now deceased victims, Sean Fentress, and move about on the train in a parallel universe. While the basic events in each alternate time line are the same (the bomber always plants the bomb in the same place), due to Stevens/Fentress acting differently in each time line, the exact sequence of events is changed. The concept derives from 'Groundhog Day', where the core events end up as variations on a theme. The interactive aspect of the protagonist's abilities, can also be likened to the Star Trek holodeck, where a user is able to interact with a series of images which are mere projections of reality.<br /><br />One can argue with all the pseudo-scientific explanations but the films' scenarists are basically asking the audience to suspend one's disbelief when it comes to the main premise. For some this is a big problem, but I found other areas of the film to take issue with. One of screenwriter Ben Ripley's big quandaries is how to develop Stevens' character arc in eight minute segments. You'll recall that Bill Murray's 'Phil' character in 'Groundhog Day' was a narcissist who needed to learn an important life's lesson about being a nice guy. He has a whole day (repeated multiple times) to transform himself. Here, we learn virtually nothing about Fentress as his back story is a cypher. When Stevens becomes Fentress, Ripley has him gradually warming up to the film's love interest, Christina Warren, after he acts gruffly toward her when Stevens makes his first 'time trip'. But Stevens/Fentress acts badly at first because Stevens is disoriented not because he has a character defect. So the transformation seems unnecessary and forced since Steven's only real goal is to prevent the bomber from causing further havoc.<br /><br />Equally less charming are the assorted characters Stevens must deal with on the train and the methods he employs to find out whether they might be the particular villain he's looking for. Again, a comparison to 'Groundhog Day' is instructive. All of the characters Phil interacts with in the Groundhog Day universe, are utterly quirky and charming. Here, they're stereotypical--either obnoxious (bad comedian) or ineffectual (computer geek). What's more, Ripley lacks the imagination to come up with a series of interesting variants. All Stevens/Fentress ends up doing is awkwardly confronting each one of his suspects by ripping the bags they're carrying and spilling the contents on the ground andgetting into some kind of physical or verbal confrontation.<br /><br />Perhaps the biggest clue that 'Source Code' lacks gravitas, is in its choice of a convincing antagonist. Heaven forbid that Mr. Ripley would attempt to create a bad guy rooted in today's headlines. Instead of perhaps an Al Qaeda sympathizer, we're presented with a Mad Bomber of the 1950s dressed up as a modern day disaffected techie. When both the bad guys and the good guys in your movie don't seem very real, the stakes aren't very high and hence the tension on the 'suspense' meter, has got to be very low!<br /><br />Finally, there's the whole question of a so-called 'twist ending' that doesn't seem to be very surprising at all. Remember, according to the rules of the alternate time line, changing events in a parallel universe, cannot change anything in the present real world. So when Stevens sends the text message to Goodwin that he's stopped the bomber and that the Source Code experiment worked, and Goodwin and Rutledge aren't even aware that any of those events have transpired, that's not the same world we experienced at the beginning of the film. Rather, it's an alternate universe where the 'happy ending' of Sean and Christina walking off into the sunset, is a reality. One other thing that did bother a lot of other people who have posted on the internet, is what happened to Sean once Stevens takes over his consciousness? I guess he's left screaming, inside the mirror!<br /><br />Back in the real world, the reality of the train bombing remains unchanged. We do wonder, after all those time jumps, did Stevens have enough time to actually communicate the information about the bomber to Rutledge and Goodwin? Couldn't the bomber have easily driven to downtown Chicago and set off his dirty bomb given how much time Stevens was taking with his jumps and inside the capsule?<br /><br />Source Code looks and feels more like a movie made exclusively for the Syfy channel. It keeps your interest in a basic way but certainly doesn't deserve all the accolades heaped upon it to date.getting into some kind of physical or verbal confrontation.<br /><br />Perhaps the biggest clue that 'Source Code' lacks gravitas, is in its choice of a convincing antagonist. Heaven forbid that Mr. Ripley would attempt to create a bad guy rooted in today's headlines. Instead of perhaps an Al Qaeda sympathizer, we're presented with a Mad Bomber of the 1950s dressed up as a modern day disaffected techie. When both the bad guys and the good guys in your movie don't seem very real, the stakes aren't very high and hence the tension on the 'suspense' meter, has got to be very low!<br /><br />Finally, there's the whole question of a so-called 'twist ending' that doesn't seem to be very surprising at all. Remember, according to the rules of the alternate time line, changing events in a parallel universe, cannot change anything in the present real world. So when Stevens sends the text message to Goodwin that he's stopped the bomber and that the Source Code experiment worked, and Goodwin and Rutledge aren't even aware that any of those events have transpired, that's not the same world we experienced at the beginning of the film. Rather, it's an alternate universe where the 'happy ending' of Sean and Christina walking off into the sunset, is a reality. One other thing that did bother a lot of other people who have posted on the internet, is what happened to Sean once Stevens takes over his consciousness? I guess he's left screaming, inside the mirror!<br /><br />Back in the real world, the reality of the train bombing remains unchanged. B008532S54|5.0|I am a 30-something person who enjoys and has had much contact, and even temporarily lived with the kind of free-living people featured in the movie, and I think this homage is pure genius!  I usually don't think much of the two main actors at all (Rudd or Aniston), but they were good in this movie.  The hippie ensemble is absolutely perfect.  Just loved this movie and if you have a love for these counter-culture types, you will probably find it to be harmless fun; a good-humored poke at hippies.  The film takes every cliche about hippies ever and squeezes them into the commune characters, but it's ok because it's self-aware that's it's just a movie; it's actually not trying to be offensive to \\"hippies\\" but in an odd way it celebrates them in a loving way! My boyfriend agrees, we both know people like the ones on the commune and believe the film presents them as sincere idealists.<br /><br />Overall, the whole movie is light, fun, is full of memorable moments and characters, has some lowbrow humor scenes (but not as overt as other movies), and has a more unique storyline than the average comedy. Like the two main characters, I appreciate that counter culture, but can't live in it full time. Usually I can't abide \\"raunchy\\" movies, but this one is actually sweet.  The lowbrow humor is more about the incredible openness of the people versus the overwhelming private-ness of the outsiders, which is a very funny juxtaposition!<br /><br />The movie is optimistic, wacky, and perhaps a bit too graphic (nudity, etc., but mostly in a non-sexual way). Are you offended by briefly watching old nudists (in a non-sexual scene)? Really? Haven't you ever seen male genitalia before? The nudity would be essential to telling the story of such a community (of which there are hundreds across America), so I don't believe it to be gratuitous in this case. These people really exist out there; I've met people like pretty much all the characters or bits of them and THEY have zero shame or weirdness about being naked (but of course, WE feel weird about it, hence the comedy).  It's just how they are, so it's all plausible.<br /><br />Some interesting flaws of the unexamined, materialistic life are pointed out throughout the film, such as how the brother basically deals with excrement to buy fancy things, like a big house--a metaphor for how the main character (and we) all live working \\"shoveling poop\\" or working at a hateful job just to buy \\"stuff\\" that makes us miserable anyway; so it's not 100% fluff--there is a message behind the laughs. 0785152458|4.0|David Lizewski/Kick Ass is being trained by Mindy/Hit Girl to become a better superhero but when Hit Girl is dissuaded from putting on her outfit and bloodying up criminals by her new step-father and recently re-united mother, Kick Ass sets off to meet others who are dressing up and fighting crime. And it turns out there's a few, so many that they wind up making the first \\"real-world\\" superhero team, Justice Forever. But things are about to get shaken up by Red Mist who is gathering an army to exact revenge on Kick Ass for his father's death.<br /><br />I remember really enjoying the first book and Mark Millar is usually an interesting writer so I was surprised to find myself not falling for this book as completely as I did the first. It might be because of a couple of things: there's a lot less humour and fun in the book, and it's very dark. Whereas the first book contained some of the thrill of a buttoned-down boy finding freedom of self through a secret identity, here he is beset by tragedy again and again. Kick Ass learns that like many costumed vigilantes, he must endure great personal suffering for his choice of putting on a mask. And these tragedies are very dark and graphic so be warned; Millar has always had a tendency to shock and he doesn't pull his punches here.<br /><br />I think the irony of the series is that Millar presented Kick Ass as a semi-plausible story of a young boy setting out to be a superhero without superpowers and thus becoming a different kind of \\"hero\\" journey not seen before, and yet time after time in this book the story falls back on comic-book archetypes and clich&eacute;s. The way the hero loses close friends and family mirrors numerous superheroes; the way the bad guy plots to blow up the city and does any number of heinous things including murder and rape; the way the hero is perceived as a villain by those he protects; by the end of the book it's no longer a singular post-modern superhero story but just another superhero story, indistinguishable from a Batman or Spiderman book.<br /><br />That said, while I found the plot a bit predictable, I began thinking about the meaning of the scenes and wondering why they were included. I think at its core, Kick Ass is a way of looking at superhero comics and their meaning on a broader scale. It could even be said of the world presented in Kick Ass that we are in the post-superhero age; where everyone wants to be and is a superhero but no-one truly is - at least not in the comic-book sense.<br /><br />Millar makes a point of defining what he believes a superhero is: it's not just putting on a costume and beating up criminals (although that is part of it) but it could also be as simple as helping out at a homeless shelter or donating blood. The fact that they wear a mask when they do these things is irrelevant. In that definition, like many things in Kick Ass, Millar is saying we can all be superheroes if we want - a better world is within our grasp even if we need to re-phrase the actions with the help of colourful outfits.<br /><br />I'm divided in my view of the book. On the one hand I feel the series has become a product of the thing he sought to parody and in the other I have the nagging suspicion that this is all designed to appear that way and that this is a truly post-modern superhero comic with many layers. On a purely superficial level, did I enjoy reading it? Sure, it's good, bloody, fun and while not all fans of the first book might like this second as much, there's enough here to make it worth your time and the fact that the book has left me pondering its meaning this much is something few comics manage. So I tentatively recommend it with the caveat that you go in with lowered expectations and with both eyes open for the meaning in the spaces in between the panels.tinguishable from a Batman or Spiderman book.<br /><br />That said, while I found the plot a bit predictable, I began thinking about the meaning of the scenes and wondering why they were included. The novel ends with the dull clich&eacute; of two lovers looking at the moon. It seems that the author grew weary of the writing task, and decided to move on, hopefully to something much, much better.<br /><br />The novel could therefore have been a lot more exciting, and perhaps with a dose of post-modern illumination its contents could be made to say something they do not. B0000TB01Y|5.0|I came to this music somewhat indirectly.  I very occasionally take in a stage musical--sometimes even on Broadway--but VERY occasionally.  WICKED, now about a decade old, was not really on my radar.  A musical based on Gregory Maguire's revisionist retelling of the OZ tales seemed like a clever enough notion, but not something that I'd rush out to see necessarily.<br /><br />But I am a member of a choral group that specializes in popular material and a few seasons ago, we did a medley of songs from the Stephen Schwartz musical that was skillfully arranged and, I found really quite compelling.  It included \\"No One Mourns the Wicked,\\" \\"Popular,\\" \\"Defying Gravity,\\" and \\"For Good,\\" which made for a piece with plenty of variety but one which also flowed together well.  And, of course, as with any piece of music you spend weeks learning, all those tunes stuck in my brain to the point where I was singing them in the car, in the shower or just about anywhere (even if they were other people's solos).<br /><br />Around the same time, I caught a PBS special featuring Kristin Chenoweth in performance and became a real fan, making sure to buy her solo CDs.  Again--for whatever reason--I put off actually purchasing the cast album.  But it was definitely inching higher up on my list.  Still, if you haven't actually seen the production, are you going to able to follow it all?  And there was the question of whether what had worked well as a five-minute medley would lose its punch when spread over more than an hour.<br /><br />There was no cause for concern, as it turned out.  True the CD did not include an actual summary, but, hey, isn't that what the Internet is for?  It DOES include a complete lyric sheet, which is helpful, but if you don't know what the nature of the \\"Something Baaad\\" (excuse me, \\"BAD\\") that was happening in Oz, for instance, you needed to consult an outside source for that info.  What also helped give me a sense of the actual production was to view the ample YTvideos from both the original and various subsequent productions.<br /><br />Stephen Schwartz's score, I gather, had garnered some criticism for various reasons, one of them being that there weren't that many \\"hummable\\" tunes included in the production.  I don't know about that.  The days of \\"Surrey With the Fringe on Top\\" seem to be long gone, but \\"Popular\\" \\"I'm Not That Girl,\\" or the more uptempo \\"Dancing Through Life,\\" are certainly crowd pleasers--and hummable enough.  Chenowith (as Glinda) and Idina Menzel (as Elphaba, the Wicked Witch)have their share of socko-boffo moments, of course--and I found myself wishing that other talented cast members like Norbert Leo Butz, Christopher Fitzgerald and Michelle Federer had a few more moments to shine, since they are obviously real pros).<br /><br />What I found especially interesting was just how varied Schwartz's score really was.  There are plenty of \\"rock musical elements,\\" especially in the pieces written for Elphaba (\\"Defying Gravity,\\" and \\"No Good Deed Goes Unpunished,\\" for example).  Idina Menzel is just the right singer for this kind of material.  She's got the dramatic sense for them--AND the big voice.  But just as appropriately, Schwartz gives the Wizard, played by song-and-dance veteran Joel Grey, numbers that are suitable for his part AND for his talent.  WICKED is almost a case study in--a historical overview of even--various Broadway styles.<br /><br />My only question now is am I intrigued enough to catch a current production of the musical.  I'm tempted,  but video clips from more recent productions don't seem to have quite the magic this 2003 cast was able to bring to the project.  That may not be fair, really, and it may just be that after listening repeatedly to the original cast, I may not be ready to see or hear anyone other than Chenowith as Glinda or Menzel as Elphaba.<br /><br />But that's the risk when the original cast was so darn good.  And so darn original.

1416550550|4.0|I really enjoyed this multi-generational family saga, especially since it involved a mystery about a character's true origins.  On her 21st birthday in 1930 Australia, Nell is told that the family she was raised with is not her real family.  She was a foundling whom no one claimed off of a ship from England.  This revelation changes the course of the rest of her life as she tries to discover who she is and where she came from.  Upon her death in 2005, her granddaughter Cassandra picks up Nell's trail when she discovers that Nell has left her a cottage in England.  As Cassandra pieces together the clues Nell has left her and other historical evidence that turns up when she arrives in England, the mystery becomes more baffling.<br /><br />The narrative often switches from Nell as a child, Nell in the 1970's, Cassandra in 2005, and the English family in question, the Mountrachets in the first decades of the 20th Century.  This constant change in perspective might turn some readers off, but I didn't have any issue with it.  I felt this style allowed the author to reveal certain aspects of Nell's mystery little by little.  Whether it's a discovery Nell makes when she visits England and purchases the cottage in the 70's or Cassandra's own sleuthing in 2005, the story slowly reveals itself, layer after layer, in a very engaging way.<br /><br />The one flaw that struck me was that the writing was not exceptional.  I found myself scratching my head, questioning certain word choices or wondering why Morton used a particular phrase, which was quite distracting.  I found the fairytales within the book contrived.  Otherwise, I really did like the structure of the novel and the characters Morton developed. She also created great atmosphere and I could visualize the vast grounds of the Blackhurst Estate and the beauty of the gardens she described. I was totally committed to the story and it kept me turning pages to discover the truth about Nell.

B005LAIHZE|3.0|I haven't read the book yet, but I have been told that the movie departs significantly from it's source. Worth noting for people who are bothered by that sort of thing.<br /><br />The core conceit of this move is patently absurd. One either accepts that going in or just doesn't go to see it. If I was to aim a single criticism at the movie though it would be that it doesn't fully embrace that absurdity.<br /><br />Sometimes it does. There are some epic moments involving silver forks being shot from a canon and young Lincoln leaping from horseback to horseback. But then it goes and gets serious and wants to talk slavery.<br /><br />Still there is a lot of fun to be had. Who doesn't want to see Abraham Lincoln decapitating and maiming the undead in as many different ways as the director can conceive?<br /><br />My second criticism is that the director gets a little too full of his artistic prowess and it's distracting. Animation is thrust into the middle of the film when it's utterly unnecessary, we get endless slow-mo segments and some scenes (particularly the horse stampede scene) where it's really hard to tell what is going on. This is not, after all, an art movie.<br /><br />Criticisms aside there is a lot of fun here too. Endless, gory, vampire killing and a deft weaving of these elements in between some historical fact. If you can suspend your disbelief suitably then watching a 50 something year old Lincoln fight vampires on the roof of a fast moving train is pretty entertaining.<br /><br />So it's a fun movie, but you'd probably be fine picking it up on DVD or Blu-Ray. It could have been better if it hadn't had pretensions to be more than it is.

B005LAII08|4.0|Yikes, Pixar gets nicked by the double-edged sword. Pixar has had such a wildly successful run of animated films, one wondered how long the streak could be sustained. I guess the uneasy murmurs first surfaced when CARS 2 was critically panned. And now here's BRAVE, and murmur, murmur, murmur. And I liked BRAVE, quite liked it. But we grade on a curve when it comes to Pixar products. And as much as I favor BRAVE, it doesn't hold up to WALL-E or UP or FINDING NEMO or the TOY STORY franchise. Any other animated studio, and odds are I would've cast a more generous eye. But Pixar's standards are so lofty... I rate BRAVE 3.5 out of 5 stars. Kids will adore this movie. The grown-ups should maybe temper expectations.<br /><br />It doesn't flaunt the originality of the Pixar classics. If anything, BRAVE wanders into the more familiar territory of Pixar's corporate partner, Disney. Set in an ancient and more mystical version of Scotland, the story revolves around Merida (Kelly Macdonald), a teenaged princess who rebels against a time-honored custom. Her doting father, King Fergus (Billy Connolly), may've erred in presenting her with a bow and arrows when she was a wee lass. Today Merida is very much a tomboy and has no use for the fineries of her status. When not besieged by her mother's unending instructions on history and proper royal decorum, Merida prefers to roam the craggy landscape and practice her archery. So imagine her collosal pique when her mother informs her that, as per tradition, the time has come for her to choose from three suitors.<br /><br />Maybe Pixar was going for irony when they released BRAVE only a week after Father's Day. The film's central conflict lies between Merida and her exacting mother, Queen Eleanor (Emma Thompson). After a hurtful argument with her mom, Merida flees into the dark woods and stumbles onto a sketchy cottage in the wilds. In the cottage she finds a woodcarving old hag (Julie Walters), except does a woodcarver's home routinely feature a surprisingly mobile broom and a creepy talking bird? Merida is desperate to change her fate. The \\"woodcarver\\" has got something for her.<br /><br />Beware the deal with the devil, the wish granted by the genie of the lamp, and the witch's spell. These never come out right for us foolish mortals. Merida's bargain leaves dreadful repercussions, and it's a bit frustrating that Merida repeatedly refuses to acknowledge any blame. The plot twist, the fallout to Merida's waywardness, is pretty unexpected - unless you knew the film's original title (which I won't mention). But, ugh... I think what follows underscores some folks' big quibble, which is that the film fails somewhat to deliver on the storytelling. As mentioned, this wouldn't be as telling if BRAVE had come out from other studios. But Pixar pictures are celebrated for their cleverness and inventiveness and rich emotional beats. BRAVE, compared to its predecessors (excepting CARS 2), presents a relatively uninspired narrative.<br /><br />I wonder what effect director Brenda Chapman's being replaced had on the film? Because, ultimately, not everything comes together for this film, and this so is unlike Pixar. There are darker, scarier moments in the film which are then offset by a brawny and obvious form of levity. Aside from Merida and Queen Eleanor's depiction, characterization for the cast is doled out in broad strokes. Not to say that I didn't like the cast. King Fergus and the triplet scamps are all endearing as hell, but they're there mostly for comic relief. But if you thought the plot thread with the three hapless suitors is going somewhere, no, it fizzles out. The visiting members of the various clans are stereotyped to the kilt. So brace up for a steady diet of spit takes, brawling, carousing, and mooning (yes, mooning). Billy Connolly, Robbie Coltrane, Kevin McKidd, and Craig Ferguson as the four contentious Scottish lairds are admittedly great fun. Again, I wish they'd been developed more.<br /><br />I know I've bagged on it, but I do recommend BRAVE. The visuals are sublime, and I'm sure it's no coincidence that your eyes are immediately drawn to Merida's fiery, untamed hair. Merida's tresses are so meticulously animated that Rapunzel's hair is gnashing its teeth (to mangle a metaphor). The Scottish Highlands are rendered in grand and panoramic detail. I love the design of the characters, the animators having leaned toward a stylized approach rather than a realistic one. Merida has got this distinctive moon face. It separates her from the other Disney princesses. And then there's Kelly Macdonald's marvelously expressive Scottish brogue. I could listen to her talk all day.<br /><br />Also setting Merida apart is her remarkable aptitude for archery. In recent months, we'd already eyeballed Katniss Everdeen and the Avengers' Hawkeye. Merida's marksmanship rivals that of those two. There's a gratifying scene in which she Robin Hoods her way thru an archery competition, but then gets chewed out by her mum for her trouble. Yeah, Merida sometimes comes across as a brat, but you never doubt that her heart is in the right place. You never doubt that this family loves each other to pieces. Were you concerned that Merida won't be able to reverse the calamitous spell before the second sunrise deadline? Me neither. Ah, but will she own up to her bad behavior? Um...<br /><br />I don't recommend viewing this in the 3D version, by the way. You just can't see anything during the night sequences, and there are heaps of night sequences. Also, you're in for a treat with the captivating animated short LA LUNA, which precedes the feature film. In it, a little boy learns the secret family business, which is awesome.><br />I know I've bagged on it, but I do recommend BRAVE. B003EYVXXW|5.0|The movie Agora centres around of the great figures in the history- the female philosopher Hypatia of Alexandria.<br />The events of the movie take place from the years 391-415 CE, and show the rise of Christianity and the fall of the old religion of the Classical World, as well as the fall of science and reason.<br /><br />Hypatia (Rachel Weisz) is a philosopher teaching science to both pagan and Christian students. She is particularly focused on one perplexing issue- the problems with Ptolemy's geocentric model of the solar system. One of her students, the future prefect Orestes (Oscar Isaac), proclaims his love for Hypatia but to his dismay she rejects him (which has much to do with the fact that her Classical Greek upbringing means she will lose her freedom if she gets married). Another who loves Hypatia is one of her family's slaves, Davus (Max Minghella), who converts to Christianity very early in the film.<br /><br />Tension rises on the streets of Alexandria as Christians constantly provoke the pagans, eventually leading to the pagans starting a massive riot in the city, in an attempt to rid it of the offending Christians. The Christians, however, are too numerous to get rid of, and the pagans must barricade themselves within the Alexandria Serapeum. Rome's Christian emperor Theodosius sends a message to Alexandria that he will pardon the pagans, on the condition that they allow the Christians to enter the Serapeum and destroy its contents. This leads to the infamous destruction of the Serapeum's vast amounts of scientific and historical knowledge and to the banning of all pagan religious practices.<br /><br />Years later, things only get worse as the monk-turned-bishop Cyril (Sami Samir) extends discrimination to not only pagans, but to Jews as well. When the murder of a group of Jews in a theatre goes unpunished by the Christian authorities, a group of Jews take the law into their own hands by killing some Christians. In retaliation, Christian mobs slaughter Jews in the streets, and the authorities do nothing to stop this.<br /><br />As well as showing the increasing intolerance created by religious fundamentalist attitudes, another important part of the plot is Hypatia coming closer and closer to solving the riddles of planetary orbits, going from rejecting Ptolemy's geocentric model in favour of Aristarchus' heliocentric one, to eventually figuring out that orbits are not perfect circles. Her support of heliocentrism (considered blasphemous to the Church at the time) and her refusal to convert to Christianity brand her a heathen and a \\"witch\\", and her influence on the prefect Orestes brand her a threat, eventually leading to her death.<br /><br />Overall, this was an excellently done movie. It was good to see a film that wasn't afraid to show historical Christian bigotry (as opposed to the usual portrayals of Christians holding some sort of moral high ground). Many Christians were apparently offended by the movie, but the fact of the matter is that Christian history is indeed very violent, and in any case the movie was criticising fundamentalism in general rather than any specific faith.<br />The characters are for the most part real historical figures, with some exceptions. The character of Davus is not a historical figure, but the way the movie is done makes this character fit into the plot quite well.<br />The film does take some liberties with historical accuracy. For instance, there is no evidence that Hypatia ever dabbled in helicentrism, let alone figured out that orbits were not perfect circles. However, seeing as very little remains of Hypatia's work, we can't rule out the possibility that she may well have made these discoveries but just never managed to publicise them. Also, Hypatia's death at the end of the film is portrayed as occurring by far less gruesome means than official accounts state (I won't give away the exact manner of her death in the film though, as I don't wish to spoil the ending for those who haven't seen the film).<br /><br />Agora is a film I would recommend to anyone with an interest in history, especially the history of religion and the history of science.

B004ZZS4CC|4.0|As some other reviewers have noted, it's really obvious that there was no professional writer involved in the creation of this book.  It's fragmented and sometimes frankly the writing is childlike and hard to follow. It's unsurprising that the writing is not always fluid -- considering the author's circumstances.  Nevertheless the voice is hers distinctly throughout this book, and that's admirable.<br /><br />I admire Jaycee because she brings two very important things to light - one, childhood sexual abuse is awful, and terrible, and inconceivable and yet - it happens.  Though the details of her abuse are difficult to read they are at the same time very eye opening.  I feel that if Jaycee is able to discuss this information publicly, it could potentially give others with the same types of painful events in their past a voice.  It's incredibly difficult to read for obvious reasons, but it also forces an acknowledgement on the part of the reader, and I think that's really important.<br /><br />Two - Jaycee is incredibly forgiving.  It gives me hope that I can forgive the small transgressions others have done me in my life and that they can forgive those I've done them, since Jaycee's obviously moving towards forgiving some major ones...the kinds of things that I cannot even conceive of forgiving...since she recognizes that not to do so would consume her inside.  This is a very mature realization and one which I presume has been brought on by years of reflection on the terrible wrongs done to her both as a child and as an adult.  All in all, this is an inspiring, bizarre, and difficult book which is basically unlike anything I've ever read before.  I certainly wish both Jaycee and her family the best - she is clearly a light in this world in spite of all that has happened to her, and that's wonderful.

B001N1DPDE|5.0|The BK2 is kind of in a category by itself; a heavy, stubby knife with a uniquely satisfying feel, that doesn't really excel at any specific task, but is rugged enough to take on just about anything from an outdoor perspective.  After having and using one for a couple of years, the carbon steel blade holds an excellent edge, and with very reasonable care, rust is a non-issue, considering that the remainder of the blade is painted.  Keep an eye on the handle bolts, however, since they will rust when the overall knife gets wet.<br /><br />The Kydex sheath is EXCELLENT, and reports of the plastic sheath dulling the carbon steel blade have no reality in my experience.  NOTE - however - the Kydex sheath is rated EXCELLENT once you have removed the big floppy belt loop and unnecessary handle strap, and replaced it with a Tek-Lok clip.  Once this sheath is fitted with a Tek-Lok, the usability of the knife goes through the roof.  One-handed draw and re-sheathing is beyond simple.  Ka-Bar should charge an extra $10 and include the Tek-Lok as part of the package; it improves the overall experience that much.  The retention of the Kydex sheath is more than adequate to keep the knife secure; the handle strap is just an unnecessary annoyance.<br /><br />Not a fan of batoning (that's what axes and saws are for), so I can't comment on the use of this knife for that purpose, but for all other heavy camping and around-the-yard uses, the BK2 has been a winner all around.  Again - when you order the knife, order the Tek-Lok with it.<br /><br />Highly recommended.

0307592731|4.0|Cheryl Strayed's \\"Wild\\" is as much a primer of what NOT to do as it is a reminder of what not to judge. The author's impetuous lack of preparation results in painful, physical tolls she pays on the PCT but the emotional healing she experiences is the dividend she receives. I found myself wanting to \\"mother\\" the 26-y-o. I wanted to warn of her of potential dangers and the strife along her path but the book is an amazing reminder that no one else can walk/hike our walk ~ it is something we have to do on our own.<br /><br />Independence, and isolation, are part of Strayed's journey as she grieves the death of her mother and the dissolution of her marriage. Along the way, she is never really alone-alone. Her mother is there with her and I found all the metaphorical allusions (as well as the very physical presence of her mother as part of Strayed) to be very moving and very comforting.<br /><br />There are some readers who will be disturbed by Strayed's brutal honesty. Her pre-PCT history of sex, drugs, rock-and-roll shaped her just as much as her time on the PCT did. As any good memoir does, she is sharing her experience from the perspective of a life that moved on from that amazing PCT hike. I know many will (and have) judge her choices. I may not agree with or condone some of the things she did but I also know that this is not my story; it is hers. I appreciate her candor, honesty, and her reflections. It is a book I am glad I read b/c I know it is one I will not soon forget.
